<h3>Project Updates</h3>


**(please enter and speak to anything interesting)**

<h4>OpenSSF Best Practices Badge project (David A. Wheeler)</h4>




* Big update, completed the upgrade from Rails 6->7 as the underlying framework on 2022-04-13. A big thanks to Andrew Fader.
* This was complicated because we use the gem (library) 'attr_encrypted' which was incompatible with Rails 7 (which adds its own encryption system). We worked with the library developers so we could use it with Rails 7 (so we could transition to Rails 7 first), and we now plan to switch to Rails 7’s encryption system (now that we’re using Rails 7).

<h4>Security Fundamentals Course SIG (David A. Wheeler)</h4>




* Adding a small item about password storage / KDFs about using a secure configuration (like minimum number of iterations) by linking to an OWASP page on this. This was inspired by the problems of LastPass & recent LUKS concerns  <https://mjg59.dreamwidth.org/66429.html>
* As always, if you have changes, please propose!
* Looking for stories (“STORY TIME”)  - things that illustrate specific issues…  That show how these things affect people in the real world & can be made clearly/succinctly.

* Call for opens (list new items below)
* Review existing guide
    * MD - [https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/blob/main/docs/Compiler_Hardening_Guides/Compiler-Options-Hardening-Guide-for-C-and-C%2B%2B.md](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/blob/main/docs/Compiler_Hardening_Guides/Compiler-Options-Hardening-Guide-for-C-and-C%2B%2B.md) 
    * Original gdoc -  [https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit#heading=h.b3casmpemf1b](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit#heading=h.b3casmpemf1b) 
    * Issue [119 ](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/issues/119)- distros compiler options 
* Review proposed pull requests on guide: [https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pulls](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pulls)
    * __GLIBCXX_ASSERTIONS ([#128](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/128))  / Precondition checks for C++ standard library calls([#142](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/142)) - New changes to review
    * gcc -v section ([#132](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/132)) - Add recommendation to explicitly enable hardening flags on the command line, rather than relying on the default profile or distro default?
    * Citations to documents calling for hardening ([#136](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/136/)) - comments?
    * Add -fno-delete-null-pointer-checks ([#139](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/139)) -  thoughts about adding this? This is being used by the Linux kernel to counter vulnerabilities that have already happened.
* Next steps

<h4>Opens</h4>




* 

<h4>Guide Notes</h4>




* Precondition checks for C++ standard library calls([#142](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/142))
    * As those features are primarily dependent on the underlying standard library and not the compiler, Thomas added references to clarify version dependencies on libc++ and libstdc++
    * CRob and David emphasize that performance impacts should be considered and described in the document
    * Siddhesh: performance impacts may be very workload specific and only noticeable in specific circumstances. We shouldn't create the misconception of significant impacts which would cause developers to not use these options. The security benefits likely outweigh the performance impacts.
    * Proposal: indicate in the overview table which options have a noteworthy performance impact and add more details in the dedicated performance impact section
* gcc -v section ([#132](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/132))
    * In previous discussions it was concluded that duplicate flags due to defaults enabled by distributions is not harmful
    * Jack: ordering of flags can have an impact on the output. Which options take precedence?
    * Thomas will add corresponding text to the PR
* Citations to documents calling for hardening ([#136](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/136/))
    * The intention is to point to supplementary documents and underline the importance of this work
    * CRob proposes to use in-text links
    * Agreement to have a dedicated discussion about the style and implementation of citations
* Add -fno-delete-null-pointer-checks ([#139](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/139))
    * David has added text on options which have been added to disable optimizations which can result in vulnerabilities in specific code bases (e.g., the kernel).
    * There are more flags like this and we should investigate those individually and add those to the document accordingly
    * Discussions to be continued
    * Siddhesh: a difference between options like buffer checks and this option here is that the latter allows the execution to continue and thereby it may cover issues. Buffer checks instead terminate the program and clearly point to a problem.

* Call for opens (list new items below)
* Review existing guide
    * MD - [https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/blob/main/docs/Compiler_Hardening_Guides/Compiler-Options-Hardening-Guide-for-C-and-C%2B%2B.md](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/blob/main/docs/Compiler_Hardening_Guides/Compiler-Options-Hardening-Guide-for-C-and-C%2B%2B.md) 
    * Original gdoc -  [https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit#heading=h.b3casmpemf1b](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit#heading=h.b3casmpemf1b) 
    * Issue [119 ](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/issues/119)- distros compiler options 
* Review proposed pull requests on guide: [https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pulls](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pulls)
* Next steps

<h4>Opens</h4>




* 

<h4>Guide Notes</h4>




* Randall noted that to be able to merge you need e-mail [operations@openssf.org](mailto:operations@openssf.org) and ask CRob to get added to the team.
* _FORTIFY_SOURCE=3 (PR [#133](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/133)) changes look good to be merged 
* Discussion on gcc -v section (PR [#132](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/132))
    * Siddhesh noted that it can be difficult for a user to tell which flags are relevant and may up the possibility for “inconsistent usage” where, based on output of gcc -v, users may assume some options are enabled by default 
    * Sam noted that gcc -v may not help with portability issues
    * It was suggested to keep the currently proposed text in the place proposed in the PR, but add an explicit recommendation to explicitly enable hardening flags on the command line, rather than relying on the default profile or distro default
    * Saddhesh proposed having an appendix focusing on configuring default profiles in GCC
    * Randall offered to work on such an appendix
* Discussion around __GLIBCXX_ASSERTION (PR [#128](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/pull/128)) and whether it would make sense to recommend filing upstream bugs of builds failing with __GLIBCXX_ASSERTION towards C++ project + whether it makes sense to have a general recommendation to report issues with hardening options upstream
    * Concerns were raised around whether such as stance could prove contentious and making a call for specific options would require making a call option-by-option, e.g., _FORTIFY_SOURCE=3 has issues with specific C language features projects may decide to use, whereas __GLIBCXX_ASSERTION usually indicates problems in user code due to invalidated pre-conditions
    * On status of PR it was suggested to incorporate LLVM libc++ `_LIBCPP_ASSERT ` into the same section, but keep options separate in the recommended options table stating one is specific for GCC libstdc++ and the other for LLVM libc++. Thomas volunteered to propose corresponding additions to the original PR.
* Randall suggested that, process-wise, it would be a good idea to tag stabilized versions of the guide periodically. Due to running out of time it was decided to bring this up on the agenda in a future meeting for more discussion.

<h3>New Friends</h3>




* 

<h3>Opens</h3>




* URL Parsers - Jonathan Leitschuh
    * There are 2 standards for parsing URLs: RFC spec + WHATWG spec (living document)
    * If you’re using a URL parser, and the thing doing the resolving is different, you can allowlist domains that resolve to something else. There was a BlackHat presentation on this topic, re: GitHub filtering. Many PHP programs will call PHP’s parser, but then call curl (which does parsing differently). Curl implements the RFC spec.
    * [https://github.com/akr3ch/BugBountyBooks/blob/main/The%20tangled%20Web_%20a%20guide%20to%20securing%20modern%20Web%20applications%20(%20PDFDrive%20).pdf](https://github.com/akr3ch/BugBountyBooks/blob/main/The%20tangled%20Web_%20a%20guide%20to%20securing%20modern%20Web%20applications%20(%20PDFDrive%20).pdf)
    * The W3C TAG might want to get involved, Dan Appelquist co-chairs. That connects RFC, WHATWG, etc. Dan A. is happy to help. [https://www.w3.org/2001/tag/](https://www.w3.org/2001/tag/)
    * It’s more complex. There are URLs, URIs, IRIs, etc.
    * Focus on _threats_ - so we can fix it.
    * [https://daniel.haxx.se/blog/2016/05/11/my-url-isnt-your-url/](https://daniel.haxx.se/blog/2016/05/11/my-url-isnt-your-url/)
    * [https://hackerone.com/reports/704621](https://hackerone.com/reports/704621)
    * Jonathan will tell us where the discussion will take place, to help id threats.
    * https://www.techrepublic.com/article/url-parsing-a-ticking-time-bomb-of-security-exploits/
* Scorecard discussion - Dan A.
    * Want to add more information from Scorecards (what’s wrong, how to fix, etc.) - probably should coordinate, so the text is the same.
    * Legitify is doing similar things.
* MS contribution to the C/C++ hardening guide (WIP)
    * The current plan is to make the MS guide separate from gcc/clang/Intel. The latter all share the same option syntax, option names for the same thing, etc. (e.g., “-” prefix for option). The MS compilers takes  different syntax & use different names, so it’s clearer to have that as a separate document (with cross-links).

* Review existing guide
    * MD - [https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/blob/main/docs/Compiler_Hardening_Guides/Compiler-Options-Hardening-Guide-for-C-and-C%2B%2B.md](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/blob/main/docs/Compiler_Hardening_Guides/Compiler-Options-Hardening-Guide-for-C-and-C%2B%2B.md) 
    * Original gdoc -  [https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit#heading=h.b3casmpemf1b](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit#heading=h.b3casmpemf1b) 
    * Issue [119 ](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/issues/119)- distros compiler options 
* Next steps

<h4>Opens</h4>




* 

<h4>Guide Notes</h4>




* Support for other compilers
    * Friends from Intel and Microsoft are joining in to complement the guide with options targeting the Intel and Microsoft compilers
* Repository permissions
    * CRob is working with LF staff to clean up and correctly set up the permissions
* Default gcc profile
    * Randall: GCC has a default profile which enables some of the recommended options if no options are specified specifically.
    * The default options may differ across distributions
    * Some options are considered "default" by the majority of distros and Linux ecosystem. Not compiling when using some of those options is even considered a bug by some.
* Siddhesh: FORTIFY_SOURCE=3 is being pushed for adoption by various distributions (e.g., Fedora). FORTIFY_SOURCE has impacts on specific language structures (e.g., flex arrays) and therefore some popular applications disable FORTIFY_SOURCE (e.g., vim).
* David: there are at least three classes of users
    * Linux distributions: this guide is probably not targeting the distribution maintainers because they already have a very good understanding of these options
    * Open source applications: we want these flags to get adopted by developers
    * Distributors / Vendors (in particular of IoT devices)
* Thomas:
    * supported versions: we should aim for covering the latest and greatest and explicitly show since which version of a compiler an option is supported.
* David: recommends to provide the latest and greatest flags and describe in the details why certain options may not be usable by specific compiler versions
* Gabriel: should we cover language constructs which may require special considerations in the context of some options?
    * This document is primarily about options, less about language construct. However, it is valuable to refer to best practices in the context of specific options.
* Discussion about enabling -O1 or -O2 when using FORTIFY_SOURCE to make it most useful.
* Randall: do we want to open bug reports in upstream projects when they don't compile when using specific options and point to this guide as a reference?
* Siddhesh: during the development of FORTIFY_SOURCE=3, the compiler devs did file bugs upstream and it generally was well received because most issues were caused by bad programming practices.

<h4>Opens</h4>




* 

<h4>Guide Notes</h4>




* Doc was rearranged and applied to markdown. The reformatting can now spark conversations on how to organize content, ex: having an introductory question
    * Created a “Hygiene Factor” for Scorecard and moved it to the end.
    * The new structure now has labels to state applicability to GitHub and/or GitLab.
    * There then needs to be applicability for each SCM.
    * Question: can the markdown generator be set up to output a structure similar to Dan’s formatting.
        * Noam, can be done. Tried to match configuration to GitHub/GitLab and the consumer.
        * The guide could be split in two or three parts - one that’s for common information and two that are GitLab or GitHub specific.
        * Do you think there may be people using both?

            Some might be - if they are managing legacy repos or migrating…

* Doc Christine shared…
    * Intended to align within F5.
    * Some confusion between github orgs and internal orgs…
    * Structured with “Operations” “OSPO” and “Project Maintainers” as Audiences.
    * Guidelines organized into repos -
        * Orgs
        * Repos	
        * Operations
    * Some intro text for each section… 
    * “Managing roles and access”
        * “Only company employees” <- it’s important if you’re a company / entity 
        * Dan: might map onto collaborators vs. external contributors
        * Process of how things become public…
            * Limit who can make repos public?
        * 2fa 
        * Inactive members and ex-employees
        * Downgrading global admin / ownerpriv for members with no activity…
    * Organizing Repos
        * Where should projects be put <- might not be relevant 
            * Make sure your orgs make sense
        * Private repos lasting longer than 6 months….
        * [should we have private repos related best practices?]
    * Repositories
        * Use “Security Scanning Tool”
    * Managing Roles & Access
        * Use teams - kinds of teams - maintain teams…
    * Project Setup & Support
        * E.g. scorecard… 
    * Operations
        * For the folks going to be operating things - turning on github insights, audit log, review security…
        * Using internal tool with advance token permission to automate tasks…
        * Different UI for maintainers…
        * Automated alerts & tools…
    * Process
        * Setting up a new github org, a new github repo…
        * Archiving a github repo… 
        * Approving 3rd party github apps…
* Discussion on best practices around teams… use of teams.
* Current thinking is to have separate GitHub and GitLab docs / markdown generated from Legitify data and have an overview (front matter) listing common best principles.
* Regenerating the markdown with specific anchors will make it easier to link to. This happens by default so should not be an issue.
* Action item:
    * Take the [GitHub Guide](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1DNJ6RszprzVuQKpYCRS6nj8j-XqcHk0Ss1w1itGbarI/edit#heading=h.35nkun2) and adapt that into the front matter to show how it could look linking to GitHub best practices.

* Welcome new friends
    * No new friends today

    * Thomas volunteered to co-lead this activity. No objections from the group.

    * [https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/blob/main/docs/Compiler_Hardening_Guides/Compiler-Options-Hardening-Guide-for-C-and-C%2B%2B.md](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/blob/main/docs/Compiler_Hardening_Guides/Compiler-Options-Hardening-Guide-for-C-and-C%2B%2B.md) 
    * Name of the document
        * David: the document should include the term "options"
        * The suggestion is valid. Thomas has already updated the draft. Current title: "Compiler Options Hardening Guide for C and C++"
    * Handling of other compilers
        * The Microsoft compiler is widely used, but different options
        * The Intel compiler is closer to gcc and clang, but there are differences nevertheless
        * Proposal: we'll focus on gcc and clang and aim for including compilers which using similar options (Intel, AMD) in a separate section of the same document. Other compilers using significantly other options (Microsoft) will be covered in a separate document.
    * Should we consider options specifically for or impacting encryption?
        * No objections, but this will probably added later
        * Depending on the content, this could be handled as an appendix
    * Navigability (Findability)
        * Add named links for details for each flag, so you can jump straight to it. <span id=”-fstack-clash-protection”></span>. That will create some ugliness in the markdown. That enables everyone to link to specifics.
        * GitHub markdown ids already work, they do change when header names
        * General idea: provide a one-sentence description of each option and link to a detailed description further down in the document.
        * David: I’ll mock up one hyperlink + anchor
    * Default configuration options per distro
        * Randall will share information about which options are enabled by default by selected distributions
* Next steps
    * Review for content - issue?
    * Review older doc, see what can be extracted
    * Randal will grab distro flag lists & share with group
    * Randal will fix the markdown formatting (in this case, markdownlint)
    * CRob reach out to Avishay to find MSFT compiler folks to contribute
    * CRob get Diana Carroll @ Intel to contribute icc flags
    * CRob lookup AMD OSSF contact and see if they are interested in contributing amd compiler options

Agenda



* Welcome new friends
* Is someone willing to scribe for the meeting?
* Upcoming TAC elections:
* **Voter Eligibility (Electorate) Self-Nomination Process**
    * Any contributor to OpenSSF working groups or initiatives is eligible to participate in the election. Valid contributions include: commits or submitted pull requests via Github; public edits or comments on Google docs or other work products associated with OpenSSF; posting messages to any mailing list or on Slack; and beyond that any other form of positive engagement with OpenSSF activities. The form asks you for an example of your contributions; this is merely to make it easier for election observers and OpenSSF staff to validate. If you have in any way been involved in or care about OpenSSF, but are in doubt as to whether your contribution “counts”, please fill it out anyways, and we will follow up.
    * Deadline: March 12, 2023
    * [Voter Eligibility Self-Nomination Form](https://docs.google.com/forms/d/e/1FAIpQLSdgQdwnRH5nMLkYUDhuM7LYxUynairprGc_xAPIZm9SGaytfg/viewform?usp=sf_link)
* **TAC Self-Nomination Process**
    * The OpenSSF Technical Advisory Council (TAC) is composed of seven total individuals, four of whom are elected annually. If you are interested in serving on the TAC, and qualify as an eligible voter as above, please complete the self-nomination form below,
    * Deadline: March 12, 2023
    * [TAC Candidate Self-Nomination Form](https://docs.google.com/forms/d/e/1FAIpQLSdn91eLxfVYwiDw4OrAw3lSYVPVqOkcTJl2VzX07Q7idONefQ/viewform?usp=sf_link) 
* **SCIR Self-Nomination Information and Process**
    * [Since early in its existence](https://openssf.org/blog/2020/10/07/openssf-seeks-security-community-individual-representative-for-governing-board/), the OpenSSF Governing Board has sought to ensure it gets adequate input from voices in the software security community who would otherwise not be at the table. We seek candidates for the Security Community Individual Representative (SCIR) who can represent those voices, while also being a subject matter expert in the field with their own set of perspectives. Familiarity with the different OpenSSF working groups and projects, and being able to dedicate the time to be sufficiently informed on the issues that arise in our monthly calls and ongoing discussions, is highly desired. It is also highly desired, but not required, that the SCIR be a contributor and thus eligible to vote in the election. 
    * Deadline: March 12, 2022
    * [SCIR Candidate Self-Nomination Form](https://docs.google.com/forms/d/e/1FAIpQLSfsbVO1wMfEXZNJmYqjm2ND929HHYgwGrNu-uRifV5xwszLCw/viewform?usp=sf_link)
* **2023 Election Process**

The Election Process will be monitored by Election Officials (volunteers identified by the TAC). At the conclusion of the validation period, Election Officials will publish the complete list of candidates.

* Begin of overall election process: February 27
* TAC Self-Nomination Period: February 27 - March 12 (2 weeks)
* SCIR Self-Nomination Period: February 27 - March 12 (2 weeks)
* Voter Ballot Request Period: February 27 - March 12 (2 weeks)
* Validation Period: March 13 - March 19 (1 week)
* Nominees Announced: March 20
* Voting Period: March 20 - April 2 (2 weeks)
* Announcement of election results: April 3
* WG update presentation for 7 March TAC call (feedback welcome!): 
    * [https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1n_zaIdwaG7Tv1UA0ZPnxl0Z7sFenKt7aB2A1vfMtgjc/edit#slide=id.g13dc63f7ee3_0_114](https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1n_zaIdwaG7Tv1UA0ZPnxl0Z7sFenKt7aB2A1vfMtgjc/edit#slide=id.g13dc63f7ee3_0_114) 
* OpenSSF Day - CFP due March 17
* OpenSSF Town Hall - March 16
    * Register - [OpenSSF Town Hall](https://email.linuxfoundation.org/e3t/Ctc/RI+113/cZw--04/VX8CZB32TpzSW1LLBFp2mhHVFW1sXT7r4XxB8mN26s60J3lLBZV1-WJV7CgBPPW341txd3NkhH1W2jy60j8pyz8lW7zjRtZ35h_4RW90phhd64Qk2bW2wphw84BKyrxW3dVF4h767v1fW4FN7fL2HzwSxW2h-YFB5zJsB3N2Zhm6glPZqFW5RGFC-9kxWSNW3Y-1xl69yKfNW4TCFd06Wn07bW4vf3VB8MtxgrW6W2bYt53BTH8VRm56-1mPhGLW445JhC3q45-2W4_TwcD5btCC5W8fJwxC4P7NYgW5f2K4m4d_L3mW42Pz021MFh3SW85W9Yq55QsJbW1LXzwJ3tfPQvW4kjTq86qdPMVN6wnjw6NNmSLVRdlpn2svDRyW65lsKn8G-PmB3b0H1)

* Some Linux distros are discussing snaps, flatpak, etc. “Next gen packaging”. They all include a bunch of things. There’s little regard on dependencies, they’re all inside, so vulnerabilities can be embedded & thus need to be constant re-issued.

<h4>Security Fundamentals Course (David A. Wheeler)</h4>




* Is HTML a target=(non-_self) still a problem? Please tell us! [https://github.com/ossf/secure-sw-dev-fundamentals/pull/108](https://github.com/ossf/secure-sw-dev-fundamentals/pull/108)
    * David: interesting discussion - if you add an <a> tag with certain characteristics it can lead to a vulnerability. The PR - browsers have made some changes and had to counter this problem. So is this is a historical problem?
    * Dan Appelquist will investigate.
    * Glenn Ten Cate: Tabnabbing. Yes it’s an issue from the past but not everyone uses latest version of browser… Older browsers would also have lots of other security issues.
    * David: old security vulnerabilities in browsers are not news. If it’s no longer relevant then I’ll cut the material.
    * Ixchel: If you don’t have the chance to update your machine then it’s relevant… 
    * David: it used to be a problem… 
    * Crob: feedback to PR108… Dan to talk to some web browser community contacts.
* Glen: what about cross site request forgery protection… Browsers also protect against this.
    * David: this is addressed already… same-site cookies which are the default.
* David: course talks briefly about side-channel attacks - timing attacks and cache attacks - it notes the existence of that. “Hey these kind of attacks exist. When selecting crypto systems take this into account.” Audience is a general app developer one.

<h3>Meeting Notes</h3>




* Dan shared his cryptpad combination of the current work from Noam.
    * Started to arrange to arrange things into categories
* Noam’s table seems to be doing similar things, aligning GH & GL options
* Members of an organization vs people who belong to a repository…
    * In a repo - people organized around teams… policies about who can be a member of the organization and who can add members…
* Up-level a bit and talk about repository access controls - and group management.
* Other systems beyond GitHub and GitLab…
* “Best Practices” vs. “Other recommendations”
* Christine to share a “generic” version of the document they have come up with in F5…
* David: any covered by scorecoards? We can have a marker in the document to indicate if there is a scorecard entry.
    * Presence of a license…
* Roy: the **repo best practices** is aligned well but there are also other asset types - like the organization, server, etc… 
* David: if something is detected by scorecards… or best practices badge… especially look for things that aren’t covered elsewhere.
* “OSSF scorecard should be above 7”
* David: work going on in Scorecard - eg. gitlab, ci systems, … 
* Dan: scorecard icon in the document where it makes sense - and a link to the documentation of that test.
* Christine: project health or status - project actively maintained… e.g. repostatus.org … active, inactive, unsupported, etc… 
    * The guidance could be “be transparent” about active / inactive status.
    * “Code is produced to show a point; showing your work is important; transparency; but no guarantee code will be maintained.”
* Christine: In topics - operations - some things you do the first time and others are on-going maintenance. Awareness of ongoing maintenance topics… How do you look into the security scans, private logs, etc… 
    * David: for a lot of OS software it’s specific libraries - and they only release source code… they aren’t responsible for production… 
    * Dan: i mean security scans, etc… 
    * Christine: “people who may be using this…” - different personas.
    * Roy: a few process related policies - github advanced security, dependency review should be enabled, secret scanning, … as long as it’s something that the SCM is supporting then it should be in scope.
    * Dan: another high-level topic…
* Personas: person who is managing a bunch of github orgs; a project maintainer; person running the operations task; responding to requests that come in “i want to be added to the organization.”; developer who is using a repo on a day to day basis
* Jay: working on a taxonomy… that spans across all things… under one umbrella… Will get a draft and circulate. 
    * **Securing critical projects** & **securing software repos** working groups… could have input…
    * David: **SLSA** folks have a number of things about source as well as build…
* “Dogfooding” - we should be making sure OpenSSF repo configuration matches… 
* Roy: we can use legitify - if you are an owner of the OpenSSF github org…

Agenda



* Welcome new friends
    * Thomas Nyman - works product security
    * Jonathan L. (Linux Foundation) - security research - interested in bulk request generation
    * Siddharth - has lots of C/C++ background

* Is someone willing to scribe for the meeting?
    * David A. Wheeler
* Call for opens (list new items below)
    * Thomas Nyman - we create guidance for internal use, we have been putting this guidance together, realized it might be useful to contribute Eriksson’s document. Ours is a starting point. It’d be nice to turn on all the flags, but that’s not reasonable - identify priorities, discuss when you should/shouldn’t. The compiler documentation can be difficult to understand. Where should it be expanded?
* Process
    * Generally we start in a Google document to allow multiple edits
    * Often then eventually switch to GitHub markdown once it’s more ready, to enable more control
    * Eriksson already has a Markdown format, the original was in Word
    * Jonathan L: There’s a live Markdown editor, I don’t remember the name. Etherpad? No - hackmd
    * If it’s already in Markdown, let’s just put it in GitHub.
* Walked through Eriksson’s Markdown draft
    * “Toolchain hardening” may be misleading, this isn’t build system protection. “Compiler protection” has the same problem. “Binary hardening” doesn’t cover static analysis.
    * Footnotes as single numbers, that’s mysterious, maybe switch to tags
    * “Not to do” - rename to Caveats? Contraindicators? [David W: I like “Caveats”, it’s a nice short word.]
    * Maybe ask gcc/clang folks what word is appropriate
    * If your OSS can’t compile with these options, that’s a problem, so they need to be compatible, so you need to TEST with these options.
    * Discouraged - context-specific
    * Make a “TL;DR” paragraph of just “all the flags” that someone can copy/paste into their build process.
    * Jonathan W - To tell when an option was added I either check the release notes for "new warning added" docs, or I `git blame` on the GCC manual sources :-) Somebody started documenting in which GCC release new warnings were added in this page [https://gcc.gnu.org/wiki/WarningHistory](https://gcc.gnu.org/wiki/WarningHistory) 
    * Separately list static analysis; binary hardening; sanitizers.
    * We could add the Intel C compiler. The Microsoft C compiler has a completely different set of option flags - if we ever did that, that should be a separate document (maybe with the same structure).
    * Languages other than C/C++ typically have a tiny set of flags.
    * Some flags are more prototypes / experimental, we only want to use stable options that are okay to use in production.
    * David W: Let’s consider starting with the Ericsson document, merge in some of the other material we have.
* Everyone: I need your GitHub id, so we can get people added.
* Next time, we’ll look at the existing draft document & any other documents/materials, so we can figure out next steps.
* Is anyone interested in co-leading this project?
* Review existing issue [97 ](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/issues/97)and early OpenSSF [guide](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit) and Eriksson’s work
* Next steps

* 
* Noam - they received a contribution that changes the wording slightly.  Currently, policy -> violation, flip to show recommendation 1st.  They will be adding unique ids for policies in the future.
* Christine - it was useful to have the priority/severity levels to understand what could go wrong so she could decide whether to implement a control.  Having examples of that what could go wrong would be helpful for implementors.
    * Noam agrees.  They have some examples of threats for some of the items.  To do more broadly, we need to understand the attack surface.  (i.e. Insider threat may elevate some items, vs. external threat).

* C/C++ Compiler options guide (currently incubating project) - [https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1SslnJuqbFUyTFnhzkhC_Q3PPGZ1zrG89COrS6LV6pz4/edit) - next steps?
    * Thomas Nyman: We have an internal document about stack protectors, etc. Developers didn’t understand what mechanisms to turn on & why they should be turned on. The documentation often isn’t really accessible (understandable) to normal developers. We call it “toolchain hardening” (OWASP’s term). They also identified which versions of which compilers support it. Note which ones only work on specific architectures.
    * This is from Eriksson, product security.
    * David: I’ve informal discussion with Fedora on this, others have talked with Gentoo
    * Randall: If you want to talk with Gentoo & gcc people, I can help.
    * Thomas: We’ve already cleared release of our document under CC-BY, we’re just waiting for double-check on our current version of the document.
    * CRob will set up meeting of interested parties. Focus: C/C++ compiler hardening options for gcc/clang, probably not other compilers with different option formats (they’re really different).
* (Proposed) Stephen Hendrick (Linux Foundation) - show what have so far, how could the WG leverage LF Research  on best research
* Linux Foundation (LF) recently started up a research group, “LF Research” -[https://www.linuxfoundation.org/research](https://www.linuxfoundation.org/research)
* We went out & did a survey of contributors about best practices
* Over 500 surveyed, only 72 completely answered survey
* Expect it to go out ~4 months
* Sample size is small. However, “maintainers” is a more rarified space, don’t expect a huge number.
* URL for deck; it’s just preliminary results, so don’t broadcast it widely (“internal use only”):

<h3>Project Updates</h3>

<h4>OpenSSF Best Practices Badge (David A. Wheeler)</h4>




* Ongoing work to upgrade to Rails 7 <[https://github.com/coreinfrastructure/best-practices-badge/pull/1908](https://github.com/coreinfrastructure/best-practices-badge/pull/1908)>.
* The hardened_website gold badge requirement requires enabling certain HTTP header entries, on both repo URLs and home page URLs. Users of GitHub automatically meet it for their *repo* URLs, however, many use GitHub pages for home page URLs, and those currently can’t meet the requirements without significant efforts. I”ve asked GitHub if this is something GitHub could support - we’ll see! <[https://github.com/coreinfrastructure/best-practices-badge/issues/1878](https://github.com/coreinfrastructure/best-practices-badge/issues/1878)>

<h4>Opens</h4>




* 

<h4>Guide Notes</h4>




* Existing guide - contributed by Noam - They have developed an open source tool that supports github and gitlab - [https://legitify.dev/](https://legitify.dev/) - and a set of policies that it checks. Could be a good base. Can generate content to make it easier.
    * [https://github.com/Legit-Labs/legitify](https://github.com/Legit-Labs/legitify) 
* How do we want to work going forward and how do we want to communicate with the community?
* Option of doing it driven by the database of rules vs. doing it by delivering an editorial document that would be based on an initial cut of the rules.
* Who is the audience from the doc: open source maintainers; someone administering the github org; different audiences. … how to continuously monitor & manage?
* Other tools that do similar functions - compare & contrast. E.g. tools for finding secrets on github.
* Secrets could be added; but right now it’s more focused on the configuration settings. Right now it’s maintained in legitify - easy to maintain. The idea is to make it easy to contribute… includes parameters for each policy. Threat level, severity, remediation steps… 
* Separate the presentation of the doc from the maintenance - process to publish a markdown file once changes are made to the database.
* Determine scope of the tool / scope of the work.
* Long and short term very clear goals:
    * We will focus on creating a consumable document that describes the practices for securing a source code management repo.
* Dan: Focus on configuration… maybe secrets out of scope?
* General agreement to focus on configuration items.
* Current legitify process - they have a database - with descriptions… they take this and generate YAML and then from that generate Markdown. Could move the policies to an openSSF repository…
* Process for donating IP and software to the OpenSSF… we could go that way.
* What is the “why” - what is the intent in producing this guide. When they pick up the guide what are they going to get from it - what are they not going get? What’s our intent? Add focus. “Outstanding source code management.”
    * We can develop a set of user stories to help address “the why” - e.g. “As an oss maintainer I need to know how to correct and securely configure my source code repo”
* Next step: let’s get an output of the current a into a folder:
    * [https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/tree/main/docs/SCM-BestPractices](https://github.com/ossf/wg-best-practices-os-developers/tree/main/docs/SCM-BestPractices) 
* Roles: open source consumer; open source maintainer; repo admin; org admin;  and user stories attached to those personas. <- vulnerabilities disclosure wg personas as a starting point
* Scope into the readme file.
* Roles into the readme file.

* Still working and brainstorming on the new SKF features. 25 labs were fixed and updated to the new styling. Fixed writeups.
* The fundamentals course will be integrated into SKF. Currently there is a manual-made integration of the course into the framework.  The plan is for SKF to figure out an HTML comment format, the comments will be added to the course, then they can just copy the text in.

    * There’s lots of interest
    * Decided to pull out into its own subgroup.
    * Intend to reach out to many other organizations, find opportunities for mentorship etc. Hopefully real impact.
    * Official invite will be going out, will send to slack/mailing list / GitHub repo
    * As always, all are welcome to participate.
    * If you’re interested in this, contact Jay & Christine.
* Intel has provided CRob a list of educational materials, will be discussing contributing some training content for the EDU SIG, CRob to follow-up
    * Maybe C/C++ compiler flags document related
